    "# Statistics 4: Multivariate Models and Choosing the Best Model"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "slide"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "## Multivariate Models\n",
    "\n",
    "Most real-world phenomena are not the result of one single cause. Consider the price of a home. It's not just the number of bedrooms that affect the price, but also the number of bathrooms, the square footage, the age, etc...\n",
    "\n",
    "Or, consider the case of predicting a baseball pitcher's career earnings.\n",
    "\n",
    "Data source: [Sean Lahman's Baseball Database](http://www.seanlahman.com/baseball-archive/statistics/)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 1,
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": false,
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "import pandas as pd\n",
    "import numpy as np\n",
    "\n",
    "baseball_dir = \"../downloads/baseballdatabank-master/core/\"\n",
    "salaries = pd.read_csv(baseball_dir + \"Salaries.csv\", sep=\",\")\n",
    "pitching = pd.read_csv(baseball_dir + \"Pitching.csv\", sep=\",\")\n",
    "rename_these = {\"G\": \"games\", \"W\": \"wins\", \"L\": \"losses\", \"H\": \"hits\",\n",
    "               \"WP\": \"wild_pitches\", \"R\": \"runs_allowed\", \"SO\": \"strikeouts\",\n",
    "               \"SHO\": \"shutouts\", \"SV\": \"saves\", \"IPouts\": \"outs_pitched\",\n",
    "               \"BB\": \"walks\", \"BFP\": \"batters_faced\"}\n",
    "pitching.rename(columns = rename_these, inplace=True)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 2,
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": false,
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "total_salaries = salaries.groupby([\"playerID\"])[\"salary\"].sum()\n",
    "total_pitching = pitching.groupby([\"playerID\"])[[\"games\", \"wins\", \"losses\",\n",
    "                                                 \"hits\", \"wild_pitches\", \n",
    "                                                 \"runs_allowed\", \n",
    "                                                 \"strikeouts\", \"outs_pitched\",\n",
    "                                                 \"shutouts\", \"saves\",\n",
    "                                                \"walks\", \"batters_faced\"]].sum()\n",
    "\n",
    "all_stats = pd.concat((total_pitching, total_salaries), axis=1)\n",
    "all_stats = all_stats[(all_stats.games > 0) & (all_stats.salary > 0)]"
   ]
  },

    "\n",
    "regressor = linear_model.LinearRegression()\n",
    "Xvals = np.array(all_stats.strikeouts)[:, np.newaxis]\n",
    "yvals = np.array(all_stats.salary)\n",
    "\n",
    "slopes, intercepts = [], []\n",
    "\n",
    "for train_index, test_index in kfolds:\n",
    "    X_train, X_test = Xvals[train_index], Xvals[test_index]\n",
    "    y_train, y_test = yvals[train_index], yvals[test_index]\n",
    "    regressor.fit(X_train, y_train)\n",
    "    slopes.append(regressor.coef_)\n",
    "    intercepts.append(regressor.intercept_)\n",
    "\n",
    "slope = np.mean(slopes)\n",
    "intercept = np.mean(intercepts)\n",
    "\n",
    "regressor.coef_ = slope\n",
    "regressor.intercept_ = intercept"
   ]
  },

   "source": [
    "This score captures the **coefficient of determination**, which tells us effectively how much of the variability of the data is captured by this model and ranges between 0 and 1. A score closer to zero indicates a model that largely fails to capture the data. Be suspicious of any score that's too close to 0 or too close to 1."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "Let's try to make this better by adding in more characteristics to our model, making our model multivariate. Note that a \"linear\" model does NOT mean that you only use one value to determine one other value, as in $y=mx+b$. It means that the independent variables $x_i$ determining your $y$ value are added to each other in a linear way, e.g. \n",
    "\n",
    "$$y = ax_1 + bx_2 + cx_3 +...$$"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 7,
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": false,
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "N_folds = 10\n",
    "kfolds = cross_validation.KFold(len(all_stats), n_folds=N_folds)\n",
    "\n",
    "regressor = linear_model.LinearRegression()\n",
    "valid_data = [\"strikeouts\", \"runs_allowed\", \"saves\", \"hits\",\n",
    "             \"shutouts\", \"wins\", \"losses\", \"outs_pitched\",\n",
    "             \"walks\", \"batters_faced\"]\n",
    "Xvals = np.array(all_stats[valid_data])\n",
    "yvals = np.array(all_stats.salary)\n",
    "\n",
    "coeffs, intercepts = [], []\n",
    "\n",
    "for train_index, test_index in kfolds:\n",
    "    X_train, X_test = Xvals[train_index], Xvals[test_index]\n",
    "    y_train, y_test = yvals[train_index], yvals[test_index]\n",
    "    regressor.fit(X_train, y_train)\n",
    "    coeffs.append(regressor.coef_)\n",
    "    intercepts.append(regressor.intercept_)\n",
    "\n",
    "coeffs = np.array(coeffs).mean(axis=0) #averages each column\n",
    "intercept = np.array(intercepts).mean(axis=0)\n",
    "\n",
    "regressor.coef_ = coeffs\n",
    "regressor.intercept_ = intercept"
   ]
  },
  {

   "source": [
    "### Take Care with Multivariate Models\n",
    "\n",
    "One thing to be aware of is that a multivariate linear model assumes that each added variable is independent of the others. As such, you want to beware of **multicollinearity**, where two or more characteristics are themselves correlated with each other (e.g. \"strikeouts\" and \"outs_pitched\"). To ensure that highly-correlated variables are not included together, one should add new variables carefully and gradually. For more, see this set of pages on [multicollinearity and other regression pitfalls](https://onlinecourses.science.psu.edu/stat501/node/343)."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "slide"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "## Choosing the Best Model\n",
    "\n",
    "When we select a model we can assume that the data is normally distributed (so, Gaussian) around some central trend that the model describes. We can evaluate that model by looking at the probability that the data we've measured would've been generated by the model that we're assuming. For a single data point $y_i$, the probability that the point would've been generated from $x_i$ and the model $\\mu_i$ is\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "p(y_i | \\,\\text{model}(x_i)) = \\displaystyle\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2\\pi\\sigma^2}}\\exp{\\left[\\frac{-(y_i - \\text{model}(x_i))^2}{2\\sigma^2} \\right]}\n",
    "\\end{align}\n",
    "\n",
    "Remembering day 2, the probability of multiple events all happening at the same time is the product of their individual probabilities. When we multiply the probability of generating each and every point $y_i$ given the model and the data, we get the **likelihood** and express that like so:\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "L = \\prod_{i=1}^N p(y_i | \\,\\text{model}(x_i)) = \\prod_{i=1}^N\\displaystyle\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2\\pi\\sigma^2}}\\exp{\\left[\\frac{-(y_i - \\text{model}(x_i))^2}{2\\sigma^2} \\right]}\n",
    "\\end{align}"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "If we can choose model parameters that maximize this likelihood, we can (in a sense) have the best model. One issue though is that because we're multiplying decimals, any result (especially for large data sets) will yield and exceedingly-small number. We can circumvent that by taking the logarithm of the likelihood, i.e. the **log-likelihood**."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "skip"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "**Aside:** The logarithm function effectively eliminates an exponential, returning whatever was within that exponential. If $y = \\exp{(x + 2)}$, then $\\ln(y) = x + 2$. One side effect is that if two numbers were being multiplied together, then the logarithm turns that product into a sum, e.g.\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "y = a\\exp(x + 2) \\longrightarrow \\ln{(y)} &=& \\ln{(a\\exp(x + 2))}\\\\ \n",
    "&=& \\ln{(a)} + \\ln{(\\exp(x + 2))}\\\\\n",
    "&=& \\ln{(a)} + x + 2\n",
    "\\end{align}\n",
    "\n",
    "So as a consequence, if $y = e^{x_1} \\cdot e^{x_2} \\cdot e^{x_3} \\cdot ... \\cdot \\,e^{x_N}$, then\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "\\ln{(y)} = x_1 + x_2 + x_3 + ... + x_N = \\sum_{i=1}^N x_i\n",
    "\\end{align}"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "-"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "The log-likelihood can be expressed as...\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "\\ln(L) = \\sum_{i=1}^N\\displaystyle\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2\\pi\\sigma^2}} - \\frac{1}{2}\\sum_{i=1}^N\\frac{(y_i - \\text{model}(x_i))^2}{\\sigma^2}\n",
    "\\end{align}\n",
    "\n",
    "No matter what model we adopt, that first term is going to be the same. We can call that a constant $C$ and proceed to ignore it. The second term looks like the $\\chi^2$ statistic we saw yesterday, which for all intents and purposes it is. We end up wanting to maximize\n",
    "\n",
    "\\begin{align}\n",
    "\\ln(L) = C - \\frac{1}{2}\\chi^2\n",
    "\\end{align}\n",
    "\n",
    "...but this is only the first part."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "source": [
    "### More Variables  Better Model"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "You can imagine that with more and more variables added to your model, you'd get progressively closer to  $R\\approx1$. However, as you try to add more characteristics to your model you risk a number of things, least of which is overfitting your data. Of course, you cross-validate to try to ensure that you don't but that's not perfect and at the end of the day you're still bound to the data you have. \n",
    "\n",
    "In practice, you want to go with [Occam's Razor](https://simple.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occam%27s_razor): \n",
    "\n",
    "    \"More things should not be used than are necessary.\" \n",
    "\n",
    "To help you stick to that, use the **Aikake Information Criterion (AIC):**\n",
    "\n",
    "$$\n",
    "AIC \\equiv -2\\ln\\left(L\\right) + 2\\,k + \\displaystyle\\frac{2\\,k\\,(k+1)}{N - k - 1}\n",
    "$$\n",
    "\n",
    "$N$ is the number of data points, $k$ is the number of model parameters, $\\ln(L)$ is the log-likelihood, and **you want to minimize this, as the model with the smallest AIC is then the best model.** Notice how the more parameters you add, the higher the AIC gets. So even if you get a better fit to the data (log-likelihood), if you just keep adding parameters, you're not actually getting the best model."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 10,
   "metadata": {
    "collapsed": false,
    "slideshow": {
     "slide_type": "subslide"
    }
   },
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def AIC(likelihood, num_points, num_params):\n",
    "    return -2. * np.log(likelihood) + 2*num_params + (2*num_params*(num_params + 1))/(num_points - num_params - 1)"
   ]
  },
